  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/2938407?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 05, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/184218-maggie-campbell" class="user" title="Maggie Campbell">Maggie Campbell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/456908.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780060929886" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer2938407">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12500977639805794098">"Dear God, let me be something every minute of every hour of my life. Let me be gay; let me be sad. Let me be cold; let me be warm. Let me be hungry...have too much to eat. Let me be ragged or well-dressed. Let me be sincere- be deceitful. Let me be truthful; let me be a liar. Let me be honorable and let me sin. Only let me be something every blessed minute. And when I sleep, let me dream all the time so that not one little piece of living is ever lost."<br /><br />"Don't say that. It's not better to die. W</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12500977639805794098">"Dear God, let me be something every minute of every hour of my life. Let me be gay; let me be sad. Let me be cold; let me be warm. Let me be hungry...have too much to eat. Let me be ragged or well-dressed. Let me be sincere- be deceitful. Let me be truthful; let me be a liar. Let me be honorable and let me sin. Only let me be something every blessed minute. And when I sleep, let me dream all the time so that not one little piece of living is ever lost."<br /><br />"Don't say that. It's not better to die. Who wants to die? Everything struggles to live. Look at that tree growing up there from the grating. It gets no sun, and water only when it rains. It's growing out of sour earth. And it's strong because its hard struggle to live is making it strong."<br /><br />"Oh, I wish I was young again when everything seemed so wonderful!"<br /><br />"Well, a person can cry for only so long. Then she has to find something else to do with her time."<br /><br />"I know that's what people say- you'll get over it. I'd say it, too. But I know it's not true. Oh, you'll be happy again, never fear. But you won't forget."</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_597679878">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/597679878" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4527753-khanh-the-grinch" class="left imgcol" title="Khanh (the Grinch)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1414813051p2/4527753.jpg" alt="Khanh (the Grinch)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/597679878?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 28, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4527753-khanh-the-grinch" class="user" title="Khanh (the Grinch)">Khanh (the Grinch)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14891.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780061120077" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer597679878">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2015521619808978462">Some books give young girls dreams of ponies, kittens, and visions of eternal love. This book is not one of them.<br /><br />If I were to make a metaphor, this book would be <strong>the equivalent of the ice bucket challenge.</strong> It offers no platitudes, it is harsh, realistic. It slaps you in the face with reality, a reality that is very rarely pleasant.<br /><br />And it is also one of the best young adult books I have ever read.<br /><br />I first read this book as a young teen, perhaps when I was 13 or 14. The main lesson I learned from i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2015521619808978462">Some books give young girls dreams of ponies, kittens, and visions of eternal love. This book is not one of them.<br /><br />If I were to make a metaphor, this book would be <strong>the equivalent of the ice bucket challenge.</strong> It offers no platitudes, it is harsh, realistic. It slaps you in the face with reality, a reality that is very rarely pleasant.<br /><br />And it is also one of the best young adult books I have ever read.<br /><br />I first read this book as a young teen, perhaps when I was 13 or 14. The main lesson I learned from it: <strong>Life is not fair.</strong> Life is hard. Life is harsh. People suffer. Good things do not come to those who wait. Even if you're the best person in the world, life can still slap you in the face, and you can only take what fate has handed you. <br /><br />Even if you strive to be the best child you can be to your parents, they can still show favoritism to your younger sibling, for no reason than the fact that your younger sibling was determined through some undetermined reason to be superior. Parents can and will play favorites, despite your best efforts.<br /><br />Even if your mother works her hands to the bone to support you and your brother, you will secretly love your wastrel, drunkard of a father more, for unfathomable reasons. Because human nature doesn't always make sense, and you can't help who you love.<br /><br />Even if you're committed to common sense, you will have your heart broken. People can and will take advantage of you, no matter how much you try to guard yourself.<br /><br />This book is a bleak one. It is about a young girl named Frannie, a child born of desperately poor parents. A quiet child, a shy child, one who takes comfort in books. I think we can all relate to that. A girl mature beyond her years, due to the hardships of the poor Brooklyn life in which she grew up, but a girl who is naive, all the same.<br /><br />She knew her family was poor, but little children never notice much of that. Her mother has to stretch a loaf of bread over an entire week, but there is magic in how she does it so that there is variety in their meals. She takes joy in playing with her brother, in getting a few pennies to buy a bit of candy at the dime store. In buying a pickle and reveling in the sourness of it. Simple joys that only children know. It is not until later in life that reality becomes all too clear.<br /><br />Her neighbors are vibrant, colorful. Above all, they are <em>people</em>. They are <em>human</em>. This may be a silly thing to note, but not all books are about people, not all books have humans that seem human. Too many books have characters who are little more than typescript on a page. The people in this book seem alive, from the grumpy old man who yells at her down the street, to the sadly tragic woman who enters into a costume competition---and wins---for wearing what judges feel to be a symbolic dress with just one arm, not realizing that she is too poor to afford both sleeves, and the one arm is from a salvaged outfit.<br /><br />If you wanted a true portrait of the people of Brooklyn in the early 20th century, you will find no better depiction in this book.<br /><br />No, this book doesn't offer any rainbows, there are no daydreams. Not all little girls need constant beauty and joy and complacency. <strong>All girls, however, need a good dose of reality.</strong> They need to know that they, too, can survive and thrive, despite what life throws at them. <br /><br />Because if a girl like Frannie can survive like a blade of grass sprouting from the hard concrete of Brooklyn, so can they.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2015521619808978462">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4419659482529678704">{Yup, I'm reading it AGAIN.}<br /><br />I sob, and I mean sob, every time I read this book. It's such a simple story--Francie Nolan is a smart little girl who's trying to find beauty in her sometimes ugly, always poverty-stricken life. Her adored father is wonderful, but too plagued by his own demons to support his family. Her mother loves her children fiercely but is often harsh because she thinks it's her job to keep them grounded in reality (oh, and she seems to love Francie's brother more). Her aunt is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4419659482529678704">{Yup, I'm reading it AGAIN.}<br /><br />I sob, and I mean sob, every time I read this book. It's such a simple story--Francie Nolan is a smart little girl who's trying to find beauty in her sometimes ugly, always poverty-stricken life. Her adored father is wonderful, but too plagued by his own demons to support his family. Her mother loves her children fiercely but is often harsh because she thinks it's her job to keep them grounded in reality (oh, and she seems to love Francie's brother more). Her aunt is a bit of a floozy, but is still kind and generous. Together, this family lives dirt-poor in Brooklyn. And that's it. But from this simple premise grows a tender, heartbreaking story. It's the only book that fills me with sadness just by thinking about it. <br /><br />Also, this is another of those books that I fear will fade away. It's just not that flashy, and it is long. I'm always saddened at how much length plays a part in what my students choose to read. Please buy it!</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_320031463">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/320031463" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2007216-peter-derk" class="left imgcol" title="Peter Derk"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1443226909p2/2007216.jpg" alt="Peter Derk" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/320031463?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 27, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2007216-peter-derk" class="user" title="Peter Derk">Peter Derk</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14891.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780061120077" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2007216-peter-derk?shelf=did-not-finish" class="actionLinkLite">did-not-finish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer320031463">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12071970180934667421">Well, the tree grows very slowly and with exhaustive detail.<br /><br />Couldn't get through this one. Actually, that's not entirely true. I could have. And I don't mean that in the way of a mountain climber who just couldn't make it to the top and then warps reality by looking back at it. No, it's more like "couldn't" as in "I couldn't eat another hashbrown from my McDonald's breakfast." Sure, I COULD have. It just didn't seem worth the pain.<br /><br />I get why this book is a classic, I think. My brother and I argue</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12071970180934667421">Well, the tree grows very slowly and with exhaustive detail.<br /><br />Couldn't get through this one. Actually, that's not entirely true. I could have. And I don't mean that in the way of a mountain climber who just couldn't make it to the top and then warps reality by looking back at it. No, it's more like "couldn't" as in "I couldn't eat another hashbrown from my McDonald's breakfast." Sure, I COULD have. It just didn't seem worth the pain.<br /><br />I get why this book is a classic, I think. My brother and I argue about this all the time. He feels like it's important to watch a movie like Casablanca because it's historically significant to the medium of film. He makes the point that without Casablanca, there is no Ghostbusters (okay, he doesn't point to Ghostbusters, but he should if he ever wants to get any traction with ME). This book is definitely of interest as a historical document. An historical document(?) You know what, I'm not done talking about Ghostbusters, so we better stick with "a historical document."<br /><br />The book is excruciatingly detailed about day-to-day life in Brooklyn during the early 1900's, down to what they had at the candy store. I shit you not, there's a page and a half describing the purchase of a pickle. The WHY of a pickle purchase. The best practices. The roles played by both seller and buyer. And here I am, enormous pickles in plastic sleeves of juice at every gas station in town. <br /><br />What I'm saying is, this is a great thing to have as it records what was happening in that time period, and also records the day-to-day life of a family that's just this side of poor. Not a war, not a huge event. Just what happens at your average Brooklyn pickle place.<br /><br />So I get why it's important, but that doesn't mean I want to read it. My brother would tell you that without Casablanca there is no Ghostbusters, and I can't disagree with that. But I like Ghostbusters. I don't like Casablanca. And with books and movies we're lucky enough to be in an age where there is more good material out there than people can consume in a lifetime. I am wholly convinced that I will never read every book that I would truly enjoy, which is messed up. Really messed up. But it's true, and that means there's really no time to waste on something that, though not terrible, just isn't doing much for me.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12071970180934667421">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_4718355">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4718355" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/288301-julia" class="left imgcol" title="Julia"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Julia" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/4718355?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 17, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/288301-julia" class="user" title="Julia">Julia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14891.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780061120077" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/288301-julia?shelf=the-classics" class="actionLinkLite">the-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer4718355">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer112110848043692768">Betty Smith's "A Tree Grows in Brooklyn" has been passed down through at least three or four generations and is highly regarded as a classic novel perfect for any young adult bent on entering adulthood and escaping from the gaping clutches of a complicated childhood. <br />While it was not for those reasons that I first picked up "Brooklyn," I came to regard it as one of the finest books that I had ever read. At first glance, it is a very deceitful book: short; words spaced nicely apart; and, a largis</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText112110848043692768">Betty Smith's "A Tree Grows in Brooklyn" has been passed down through at least three or four generations and is highly regarded as a classic novel perfect for any young adult bent on entering adulthood and escaping from the gaping clutches of a complicated childhood. <br />While it was not for those reasons that I first picked up "Brooklyn," I came to regard it as one of the finest books that I had ever read. At first glance, it is a very deceitful book: short; words spaced nicely apart; and, a largish font size. However, as I began to become more enveloped in the life of a young Brooklyn girl dreaming of becoming big, I realized that this tale was not as easy as the superficial first glance had led me to believe.<br />For one, Francie's sufferings and trials from being the unloved child gave me a special, odd sort of comfort. If she could survive-no, flourish-living in the slums of Brooklyn with a drunk Irish father and a mother who was not always there for her, why could I not do so in absolute comfort? (Granted, my father is not a drunk, nor is he Irish; and my mother is always there for me. Still, as every young adult feels at one point during this trying time, I have often thought that there was no one to whom I could turn for steady support)<br />Secondly, Betty Smith wrote the novel in a fluid, page-turning manner. Her every word supports and encourages the next, while also performing the duty of enticing the reader to keep marching onward. She writes simply and plainly, a very modern woman in a time where their position in society was shifting.<br />She created in Francie a heroine worthy of comparison to Jane Austen's beloved Elizabeth Bennet or Elinor Dashwood. Bold, daring, smart, and at the same time reserved, wise, creative, and thoughtful, Smith wrote a protagonist not only for the shifting ways of the early 20th century, but for all time. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="112110848043692768">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/193255-sarah-null" class="user" title="Sarah Null">Sarah Null</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/482976.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780060736262" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/193255-sarah-null?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/193255-sarah-null?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/193255-sarah-null?shelf=american-as-apple-pie" class="actionLinkLite">american-as-apple-pie</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/193255-sarah-null?shelf=bildungsroman" class="actionLinkLite">bildungsroman</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/193255-sarah-null?shelf=book-club-rg" class="actionLinkLite">book-club-rg</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer6278835">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13849029139963129712"><em>Francie stood on tiptoe and stretched her arms wide. "Oh, I want to hold it all!" she cried. "I want to hold the way the night is - cold without wind. And the way the stars are so near and shiny. I want to hold all of it tight until it hollers out, 'Let me go! Let me go!'"</em><br /><br />The title of this novel refers to a tree that grows persistently up through the concrete and harsh conditions of a poor tenement neighborhood in early 1900s Brooklyn. But it is also a metaphor for the novel's protagonist, Franc</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13849029139963129712"><em>Francie stood on tiptoe and stretched her arms wide. "Oh, I want to hold it all!" she cried. "I want to hold the way the night is - cold without wind. And the way the stars are so near and shiny. I want to hold all of it tight until it hollers out, 'Let me go! Let me go!'"</em><br /><br />The title of this novel refers to a tree that grows persistently up through the concrete and harsh conditions of a poor tenement neighborhood in early 1900s Brooklyn. But it is also a metaphor for the novel's protagonist, Francie Nolan. She is a sweet, innocent girl who grows and flourishes despite a harsh environment of neglect and poverty.<br /><br />I fell in love with Francie. I loved her childlike innocence and the way she could be so delighted with things we take for granted: things like a flower in a brown bowl, freshly sharpened pencils, dancing shadows on her pillow, shiny stars. I love her pluckiness; I loved the way she refused to conform to the mold her teacher tried to force on her, the way she pulls herself out of poverty by working hard, even though it means giving up on some dreams.<br /><br />But the novel is about so much more than just Francie. This is a beautifully moving portrayal of the human condition and the plight of the downtrodden, similar to the work of Steinbeck, though more hopeful. There is so much American pride coming from the point of view of poor immigrants and their children. The heroes of this book are not great men. They are ordinary people. They are flawed. And they are beautiful.<br /><br />I just want to hold all of them tight until they holler out, "Let me go! Let me go!"</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13849029139963129712">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_353728553">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/353728553" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="left imgcol" title="Nataliya"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1395089173p2/3672777.jpg" alt="Nataliya" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/353728553?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 02, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="user" title="Nataliya">Nataliya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5487030-a-tree-grows-in-brooklyn" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=awesome-kickass-heroines" class="actionLinkLite">awesome-kickass-heroines</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=for-my-future-hypothetical-daughter" class="actionLinkLite">for-my-future-hypothetical-daughter</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2013-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2013-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=location-is-the-true-protagonist" class="actionLinkLite">location-is-the-true-protagonist</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer353728553">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4374587883253494500"><br /><em>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</em> is a quiet, gentle, understated and yet at the same time unexpectedly scathing at times book that offers a window <em>(or a view from a fire escape, if you please)</em> into a little corner of the world a century ago, and yet still has the power to resonate with readers of today. <br /><br /><strong>After all, the world has moved forward, yes, but the essential human soul remains the same, and the obstacles in human lives - poverty, inequality, cruelty, and blind self-righteousness - are in no dange</strong></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4374587883253494500"><br /><em>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</em> is a quiet, gentle, understated and yet at the same time unexpectedly scathing at times book that offers a window <em>(or a view from a fire escape, if you please)</em> into a little corner of the world a century ago, and yet still has the power to resonate with readers of today. <br /><br /><strong>After all, the world has moved forward, yes, but the essential human soul remains the same, and the obstacles in human lives - poverty, inequality, cruelty, and blind self-righteousness - are in no danger of disappearing.</strong><blockquote>
  <em>"The one tree in Francie’s yard was neither a pine nor a hemlock. It had pointed leaves which grew along green switches which radiated from the bough and made a tree which looked like a lot of opened green umbrellas. Some people called it the Tree of Heaven. No matter where its seed fell, it made a tree which struggled to reach the sky. It grew in boarded-up lots and out of neglected rubbish heaps and it was the only tree that grew out of cement. It grew lushly, but only in the tenements districts."</em>
</blockquote><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380232289i/681312._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />It's not easy to answer what this book is about, to answer it in a way that would manage to capture the heart and soul of this story. <strong>If you ask me, I think it's a story of people simply being people, the good-bad-and-ugly of humanity. </strong><br /><br />There are so many things coexisting in the pages of this not-that-long book. On one hand, it's a classic coming-of-age and loss-of-innocence tale centered around the experiences of a young girl growing up in Brooklyn in the first couple of decades of the 20th century. On another hand, it is a social commentary taking on the uglier parts of human lives and human nature - the parts that Francie was cautioned against writing about as they are quite 'sordid': poverty, vice, exploitation, intolerance. On yet another hand <em>(yes, I'm running out of hands here)</em> it's a story of American dream - hopeful and determined. <blockquote>
  <em>“I want to live for something. I don't want to live to get charity food to give me enough strength to go back to get more charity food.”</em>
</blockquote>On a different hand, it is also a story of how American dream can be used exactly against the same people that it's supposed to inspire. On yet another hand <em>(apparently my 'hands' example may as well involve an octopus)</em> it is a chronicle of a struggling Brooklyn family with the love and resentment and strong ties that only the members of the family can try to understand. On some other hand, it's a story of what it meant to be a girl and then a woman in the world of a century ago in America. And, on yet another hand, it is an ode to Brooklyn that through the prism of this book appears to be a universe of its own.<br /><br />It is also a story of opportunities lost and opportunities gained despite the odds. It's a story about the will to survive no matter what, about iron-clad will and determination, about hope despite the odds, despite being, for all intents and purposes, on the bottom of the barrel. It's a story about learning to love and respect and compromise and give up - and frequently all at the same time. It's a story about being able to open your eyes to the world around you as you grow up and learning to see this world for what it is, and accept some of it, and reject some, too. It has love and loss and pain and happiness and wonder and ugliness - all candidly and unapologetically presented to the readers allowing them to arrive at their own conclusions just as Francie Nolan has arrived at hers.<br /><br /><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380232289i/681313._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Apparently when this book was published in mid-1940s, it caused a wave of disappointment and disagreement with the subject matters it raised, <strong>the subject matters that some of the public, like the well-meaning but clearly clueless teacher Miss Garnder in this book, probably found too 'sordid' for their taste:</strong> the poverty, the pro-union message, the lack of condemnation of female sexuality, the alcoholism, the treatment of immigrants unfamiliar with their rights, the exploitation of the poor and weak ones by those in power - you name it. It seems there was too much of the social message presented with not enough of polishing it and coating it with the feel-good message.<blockquote>
  <em>"In a flash, she saw which way the wind blew; she saw it blew against children like Francie."</em>
</blockquote>The part that probably resonated the most with me out of everything I mentioned, however, was the way Betty Smith describes the poverty of Francie's family and Francie's neighborhood (<em>"... in the Nolan neighborhood, if you could prove you had been born in America, it was equivalent to a Mayflower standing"</em> and where <em>"Kids grow up quick in this neighborhood."</em>) - the area populated mostly by immigrants not quite aware of their rights, selling their votes for the chance to survive another day, and slaving at their jobs just to survive another day in which they can go on slaving for pennies to survive. <strong>And yet the system - as well as the still-not-understood undershades of human psyche - instead of uniting these people in their hardships ends up somehow pitting them against each other.</strong><blockquote>
  <em>"She had been in school but half a day when she knew that she would never be a teacher’s pet. That privilege was reserved for a small group of girls... girls with freshly curled hair, crisp clean pinafores and new silk hairbows. They were the children of the prosperous storekeepers of the neighborhood. Francie noticed how Miss Briggs, the teacher, beamed on them and seated them in the choicest places in the front row. These darlings were not made to share seats. Miss Briggs’s voice was gentle when she spoke to these fortune-favored few, and snarling when she spoke to the great crowd of unwashed."</em>
</blockquote>You see, the poverty presented in this book, the poverty in which the Nolan family lives, is far from the innocent, idealistic, noble and 'cleansing' way it's often presented. <strong>No, this book does not fall into the pitfall of somehow glorifying poverty.</strong> The Nolans are decent people DESPITE their poverty and not in any way thanks to it - the message that is presented subtly but clearly through Francie's understanding that there's little point to it, that there's really nothing to be gained from it no matter how you can later justify it to yourself through the idea that 'what doesn't kill you makes you stronger'¹.<blockquote>Allow me to quote Terry Pratchett here:<br /><br /><em>-"Remember - that which does not kill us can only make us stronger."<br />"And that which *does* kill us leaves us dead!"</em>
</blockquote><strong>And, of course, denigration of poor people and worship of money, as well as the stark gap between the rich and the poor in the American society did not go away a century after the events of this novel.</strong> Neither did the fact that if you live in a poor neighborhood and get an education there, you are at a disadvantage as compared to your peers (Francie tried to combat that by finding a way to attend a better school in a better area - but using the ways that would surely condemn her in the eyes of the general public had she done it now, like quite a few people try to). And the fact that as we continue to proclaim the benefits of Democracy (as Johnny Nolan did his whole short life) while poverty continues to run rampant and the rich continue to be rich is perhaps one of the saddest things that you take from reading this book.<blockquote>
  <em>"They think this is so good," she thought. "They think it’s good— the tree they got for nothing and their father playing up to them and the singing and the way the neighbors are happy. They think they’re mighty lucky that they’re living and that it’s Christmas again. They can’t see that we live on a dirty street in a dirty house among people who aren’t much good. Johnny and the children can’t see how pitiful it is that our neighbors have to make happiness out of this filth and dirt. My children must get out of this. They must come to more than Johnny or me or all these people around us."</em>
</blockquote>Another part is the deconstruction of American Dream - to a point. On one hand, Francie and her mother Katie and her grandmother Mary all support the idea of education eventually being able to help you get out of the cycle of poverty. On the other hand, through Francie's eyes we see the flipside of this believe in American Dream - the shrugging off the problems of the poor by those who are a bit more well-to-do under the mistaken beliefs that (a) they understand exactly what the poor are going through <em>(like Francie's teacher Miss Garnder 'understood' poverty because - oh the horror! - at some point in her life she lived on tea and toast for three days and her family did not always have a maid)</em> and (b) assume that the only reason the poor stay poor is because they have to be lazy <em>(again, like Miss Garnder, the well-meaning soul who nevertheless was in position of power to pass on her flawed beliefs to the impressionable young children she educated)</em>.<blockquote>
  <em>“But poverty, starvation and drunkenness are ugly subjects to choose. We all admit these things exist. But one doesn’t write about them.” <br />“What does one write about?” Unconsciously, Francie picked up the teacher’s phraseology. <br />“One delves into the imagination and finds beauty there. The writer, like the artist, must strive for beauty always.” <br />“What is beauty?” asked the child. <br />“I can think of no better definition than Keats’: ‘Beauty is truth, truth beauty.’” <br />Francie took her courage into her two hands and said, “Those stories are the truth.”</em>
</blockquote><strong><img width="350" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380232289i/681314.jpg" /><br /><br />This book is simply written and slow-moving - but in an enchanting, engrossing way that allows the characters to shine through its pages. </strong>There's really little plot in the way we, modern readers, frequently think of such. Most of the book seems to be comprised of little vignettes connected to each other, placed to shed light on different aspects of the lives of the Nolans and the Rommelys, to present different edges of their personalities and to show the wider picture of the time and the neighborhood where they live. We get to experience Katie's determined strength, Johnny's unabashed hopefulness mixed with weakness, Sissy's love and disregard for arbitrary societal limitations, and Francie's curiosity and desire for life and learning.<blockquote>
  <em>"Everything struggles to live. Look at that tree growing up there out of that grating. It gets no sun, and water only when it rains. It’s growing out of sour earth. And it’s strong because its hard struggle to live is making it strong. My children will be strong that way."</em>
</blockquote>And a word about Francie herself, of course. Yes, she is far from an ideal heroine. She is naive and impressionable, sometimes frustratingly so. She can be meek and allow others to take advantage of her and direct her life - to the point when we, readers from the time when women can vote and have achieved some resemblance of equality, start getting frustrated with her. <strong>But she has this insatiable curiosity for life and desire to rise above her low station in life, and inner backbone and character steel that she appears to have inherited from her mother Katie</strong> <em>(Katie, who is a true cornerstone of this book, the source of its inner strength and resilience that allows the Nolans to have hope for the future)</em> - all the traits that make the reader cheer for this quiet and yet determined young woman who will ultimately find out what's best for her in life while always remembering where she comes from.<blockquote>
  <em>“Dear God," she prayed, "let me be something every minute of every hour of my life. Let me be gay; let me be sad. Let me be cold; let me be warm. Let me be hungry...have too much to eat. Let me be ragged or well dressed. Let me be sincere - be deceitful. Let me be truthful; let me be a liar. Let me be honorable and let me sin. Only let me be something every blessed minute. And when I sleep, let me dream all the time so that not one little piece of living is ever lost.” </em>
</blockquote>I'm glad I read this book now and not back when I was a kid. Back then I would have judged so many characters harshly, seeing the world from a quite privileged perspective of a person who had the luxury of education and only experienced a few years of significant poverty that was followed by a reasonably comfortable life afterwards. Now, with a bit more life experience on my shoulders, I cannot help but adore the quiet heart of this story and the different shades of life and people that it portrays. <strong>4.5 stars without a bit of hesitation.</strong></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4374587883253494500">...more</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/482976.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780060736262" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">High school students, U.S. history classes</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Sparrow by: </span>
      Amanda Coleman
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1302775-sparrow?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1302775-sparrow?shelf=classic-young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">classic-young-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1302775-sparrow?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer26450723">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10218736767284786808">It is a tribute to Jeanette Walls that I could not get through this book without comparing it dozens of times to <a title="The Glass Castle by Jeannette Walls" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7445.The_Glass_Castle">The Glass Castle</a>, with <em>The Glass Castle</em> coming off as its genius granddaughter or fashionable little sister. I probably should have read this first, as a child or teenager, but it’s too late for that now. No regrets! I could not help wondering why Betty Smith wrote this story as fiction rather than memoir, and the fact of it being fiction made me notice a lack of complexity in Francie</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10218736767284786808">It is a tribute to Jeanette Walls that I could not get through this book without comparing it dozens of times to <a title="The Glass Castle by Jeannette Walls" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7445.The_Glass_Castle">The Glass Castle</a>, with <em>The Glass Castle</em> coming off as its genius granddaughter or fashionable little sister. I probably should have read this first, as a child or teenager, but it’s too late for that now. No regrets! I could not help wondering why Betty Smith wrote this story as fiction rather than memoir, and the fact of it being fiction made me notice a lack of complexity in Francie’s character. Smith did not love, admire, and criticize Francie in the same way she did the Rommely sisters or Johnny Nolan. I am sure that it is because, although Smith uses the omniscient third person, Francie <em>is</em> Smith, and the story is thoroughly from Francie’s point of view. It is difficult to treat yourself as a fictional character. At the same time, the comparison of the two books is also a tribute to Jeanette Walls because <em>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</em> is a very wonderful book with many, many beautiful moments. I enjoy photographs that take something ordinary or dreary in real life and turn it into something interesting and beautiful, and this book is the written equivalent of that.<br /><br />There is a section of this story when Francie meets with her English teacher, in which Smith states one of her theories on writing, and it has stuck with me. Always an exceptional writer, Francie has recently stopped writing romantic, idealized descriptions of things she’s never seen, and begun writing stories about her father’s alcoholism. Her teacher dislikes these stories and tells Francie that successful writing is always about something beautiful and better than life. This is a major conflict for Francie because her father was a beautiful, better-than-life person to her despite his alcoholism, and she feels her teacher’s judgment of their poverty. She also finds that once she has begun writing about real things, it would be superficial to write about anything else.<br /><br />This exchange was thought-provoking for me because I generally land on the side of Francie’s teacher in this argument. I read for pleasure, and so when an author seems absolutely bent on being vulgar and unpleasant, it makes me angry. I like for fiction to be beautiful and better than life. At the same time, Smith made me realize that my argument is a myopic generalization. Smith’s descriptions of the Nolan family’s poverty and Johnny Nolan’s alcoholism are beautiful and delicate, even though the facts of both are not beautiful or delicate. The descriptions are even important, because it is so easy to oversimplify classes of people into noble or lazy, rather than seeing the complexity of individual situations. I’m glad that Smith did not take her English teacher’s and my advice. <br /><br />While I enjoyed most of this book, I did not love it. I think this was because I did not love Francie, or even have a very definite image of who she was. I <em>loved</em> all of her family members and the stories of their lives. I found the Rommely family wonderful and fascinating, even Katie’s evil father. I would never argue that this was not an important book, and I am glad I read it. As fiction and even as a coming of age story, there was not a specific plot point drawing me through the book, as most of the events were pretty well foretold from the first 100 pages. I do not think this was a failing on Smith’s part, because I believe her intention was more photographic – a series of snap shots of life in Brooklyn before World War I. I am looking forward to watching the movie, though, as I think I will benefit from having a face for Francie. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10218736767284786808">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/249999724?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 25, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/83144-el" class="user" title="El">El</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/482976.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780060736262" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-early" class="actionLinkLite">20th-centurylit-early</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=peer-pressure" class="actionLinkLite">peer-pressure</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=young-adult-n-kids" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult-n-kids</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer249999724">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer189698183396025036">I felt like the last person in the world to have read this book, and based on what everyone has said about it over the years, I expected this to be the next best thing after the Crispy Potato Soft Taco at Taco Bell. But as I read the first 200 pages, I thought everyone was out of their freaking minds. <em>This</em>, I thought, <em>is what everyone has been raving about for as long as I can remember?</em> I even did a quick peek at my GR friends list - you people love this book. I couldn't figure out why.<br /><br />It starte</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText189698183396025036">I felt like the last person in the world to have read this book, and based on what everyone has said about it over the years, I expected this to be the next best thing after the Crispy Potato Soft Taco at Taco Bell. But as I read the first 200 pages, I thought everyone was out of their freaking minds. <em>This</em>, I thought, <em>is what everyone has been raving about for as long as I can remember?</em> I even did a quick peek at my GR friends list - you people love this book. I couldn't figure out why.<br /><br />It started coming together for me somewhere after the 200-page mark. Things actually started happening, and the chapters weren't just excuses to explain some sort of mundane aspect of Francie's life. I don't need a lot of melodrama in my literature, but there needs to be some sort of conflict. Some sort of obstacle to overcome. Some sort of <em>tension</em>. This book lacked that for a good portion of the story.<br /><br />When things did get interesting, I started to understand why so many people love this book. Personally I don't love it. It didn't make me weep, though I admit to tearing up maybe once. I think this is another one of those books that I should have read when I was much younger to have a full appreciation for this coming-of-age novel.<br /><br />I can appreciate it for what it is. But it didn't change my life.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="189698183396025036">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1010385914">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1010385914" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/19832827-b-the-bookaddict" class="left imgcol" title="B the BookAddict"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1452453678p2/19832827.jpg" alt="B the BookAddict" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1010385914?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 12, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/19832827-b-the-bookaddict" class="user" title="B the BookAddict">B the BookAddict</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14891.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780061120077" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Most readers</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to B the BookAddict by: </span>
      Anastasia
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/19832827-b-the-bookaddict?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1010385914">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1339309354484565235"><br />A Tree Grows In Brooklyn is the story of Francie (Frances) Nolan growing up in the slums of Brooklyn in 1912. Over the years, it has been called a timeless classic, a description which will undoubtedly remain steadfast.<br /><br /><em> “Francie’s mother is small and pretty but steely and tough but her father is warm and charming and, above all, a prisoner of his need for drink.</em>”* Kate, the mother, is the breadwinner of the family, clean houses for the money, which feeds, clothes and keep the family warm (when t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1339309354484565235"><br />A Tree Grows In Brooklyn is the story of Francie (Frances) Nolan growing up in the slums of Brooklyn in 1912. Over the years, it has been called a timeless classic, a description which will undoubtedly remain steadfast.<br /><br /><em> “Francie’s mother is small and pretty but steely and tough but her father is warm and charming and, above all, a prisoner of his need for drink.</em>”* Kate, the mother, is the breadwinner of the family, clean houses for the money, which feeds, clothes and keep the family warm (when the money stretches that far). Kate favours Neeley, the son, while Johnny, the father, provides the love and understanding Francie needs. Francie is a lonely child who collects junk for pennies to help supplement the family’s income. She has no friends, her companion is Neeley (when he's around) and a beloved aunt. <br /><br />She longs for school but school, when she gets there, is not the place she has imagined. It’s tough and cruel, the teachers are harsh, and Francie suffers many indignities. But it provides the outlet she requires, she learns to read and her love of reading and writing is born. It is here that she decides she will become first, a writer, then a playwright. It is at school she also learns the spitefulness of girls, and vows <em>“I will never have a woman friend</em>”. At the end of grade school, her beloved father’s death means that Francie must finish school and go to work; her first two jobs are factory work, followed by office work in New York; across the Brooklyn Bridge and a place Francie has always longed for. Francie’s story and that of her family’s continues well into her late teens. <br /><br />Perhaps the impact of this story is that the reader, as well as Francie, says goodbye to her young self at the novel’s close. Personally, I was deflated to learn there was no sequel; I loved Francie and felt I had grown up in Brooklyn with her. I wanted more; what happened to Francie, where did she go, who did she love (if she loved), did she become a playwright. Quindlen says there is no doubt that this is a memoir, rewritten at the behest of editors, as a fictional story. So probably I do know what happened in Francie’s later life although I still wanted to read it, wanted to hear it in Francie’s words.<br /><br /><a title="Anna Quindlen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3500.Anna_Quindlen">Anna Quindlen</a>, in her wonderful introduction says: <em>“It is not a showy book from a literary point of view. It’s pages are not larded with metaphor or simile or the sound of the writer’s voice in love with it’s own music. Its glory is in the clear-eyed descriptions of its scenes and people.” [ ]“This is that rare and enduring thing, a book in which, no matter what our backgrounds, we recognise ourselves.</em>”<br /><br />Quindlen's last sentence is so true: I found Francie’s story waking some forgotten memory in me – until that is, I reminded myself that I grew up in a upper middle-class family in Melbourne in the middle 1960s. I am pleased to find that people of all ages are still reading Smith’s wonderful novel. I think it is a story that remains relevant no matter the year or your age. It will always remain one of my most treasured reads. Thanks to Anastasia for recommending the book to me. Most Highly Recommended. 4.5? <br /><br />*Quoted from Quindlen’s Introduction.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1339309354484565235">...more</a>

          </span>

              <a href="/review/list/1397575-daniella?shelf=to-buy" class="actionLinkLite">to-buy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1397575-daniella?shelf=rereadable" class="actionLinkLite">rereadable</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1397575-daniella?shelf=2011" class="actionLinkLite">2011</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1397575-daniella?shelf=coming-of-age" class="actionLinkLite">coming-of-age</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer29294243">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12315405151322693269"><em>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</em> is my absolute favorite book of all time.<br /><br />While the story is set at the turn of the century (1902-1919) and contains many historical elements that may feel alien to the modern reader, the message that is subtly and intricately woven into the fabric of the story is one that I feel not only transcends the ages, but also one with which many of us can identify.<br /><br />The protagonist, Francie, and her family represent the sort of wonderfully complex characters who come alive in the r</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12315405151322693269"><em>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</em> is my absolute favorite book of all time.<br /><br />While the story is set at the turn of the century (1902-1919) and contains many historical elements that may feel alien to the modern reader, the message that is subtly and intricately woven into the fabric of the story is one that I feel not only transcends the ages, but also one with which many of us can identify.<br /><br />The protagonist, Francie, and her family represent the sort of wonderfully complex characters who come alive in the reader's mind as fully as if they were old friends. Detractors say that Francie fits the depressing Pauper archetype, who spends the vast majority of the book being beaten down by her unfortunate circumstances. For me, however, she unfolds into a delightful character who is easy to love; a heroine who strikes a delicate balance between sinner and saint, full of humor, wit, compassion, strength, imagination and a unique perspective on the world around her.<br /><br />Altogether, <em>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</em> is a fantastic book that is engrossing, evocative, poignant and inspiring.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12315405151322693269">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/319909791?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 09, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3533262-stacey-prettybooks" class="user" title="Stacey (prettybooks)">Stacey (prettybooks)</a>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/847743.A_Tree_Grows_In_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780749313784" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Stacey by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4731097-priscilla">Priscilla</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer319909791">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3678690761964682980"><em>This post is part of the <a rel="nofollow" href="https://theprettybooks.wordpress.com/2014/12/27/2015-classics-challenge/" title="2015 Classics Challenge">2015 Classics Challenge</a>.</em><br /><br /><strong>WHEN I Discovered This Classic</strong><br />I was watching a video by Priscilla at <a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Q-Wl7EIM9dg">The Readables</a> when I came across it. Priscilla gave it an amazing review and so I asked for it for Christmas – over 3 years ago!<br /><br /><strong>WHY I Chose to Read It </strong><br />I chose to read it because I've had it for <em>so</em> long that it was about time. And this year I discovered that it was one of the most popular books on my TBR, much to my surprise.<br /><br /><strong>WHAT Makes It A Classic</strong><br /><em>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</em> probably</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3678690761964682980"><em>This post is part of the <a rel="nofollow" href="https://theprettybooks.wordpress.com/2014/12/27/2015-classics-challenge/" title="2015 Classics Challenge">2015 Classics Challenge</a>.</em><br /><br /><strong>WHEN I Discovered This Classic</strong><br />I was watching a video by Priscilla at <a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Q-Wl7EIM9dg">The Readables</a> when I came across it. Priscilla gave it an amazing review and so I asked for it for Christmas – over 3 years ago!<br /><br /><strong>WHY I Chose to Read It </strong><br />I chose to read it because I've had it for <em>so</em> long that it was about time. And this year I discovered that it was one of the most popular books on my TBR, much to my surprise.<br /><br /><strong>WHAT Makes It A Classic</strong><br /><em>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</em> probably doesn't count as a classic in the UK because it's a very American story. Nonetheless, I'm sure children grew up in similar circumstances in east London in the early 1900s, too. I can see why it's treasured across the pond. I wish it was as popular here because even though the historical setting may not be the same, the colourful characters go beyond time and place. It's a classic coming-of-age novel; a story that's quiet and yet full of life.<br /><br /><strong>WHAT I Thought of This Classic</strong><br />It's my first 5* classic of the year (excluding <em>
  <a title="To Kill a Mockingbird by Harper Lee" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2657.To_Kill_a_Mockingbird">To Kill a Mockingbird</a>
</em>, which was a re-read). Hooray! I was starting to worry that there wouldn't be one this year but <em>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</em> is sensational. I adored reading about young Francie Nolan, growing up as a ferocious reader (quite a Matilda-like character) and, later, an aspiring writer. It was both painful and heartwarming to hear about the troubles that her family go through, from her aunt Sissy's miscarriages to her father Johnny's alcoholism, and not forgetting her mother Katie's determination and strength. Often Katie feels like a woman in her 40s because she's been through so much, but she's about my age! <em>A Tree Grows in Brooklyn</em> is an utterly wonderful and unforgettable tale of family and growing up.<br /><br /><em>“Oh, magic hour, when a child first knows she can read printed words.”</em><br /><br /><strong>WILL It Stay A Classic</strong><br />It's a beloved book in the USA and I'm sure it'll be read for many years to come – Francie's tale will never stop being poignant.<br /><br /><strong>WHO I’d Recommend It To</strong><br />People who love young adult and children's fiction. People who love slow (but not boring!) stories. People who love stories about people – especially bookish characters!<br /><br /><em>“And always, there was the magic of learning things.”</em><br /><br /><br /><em>I also reviewed this book over on <a rel="nofollow" href="http://theprettybooks.wordpress.com/book-reviews/">Pretty Books</a>.</em></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3678690761964682980">...more</a>

        rated it

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer391706677">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6488433209914403781"><em>OH WHAT A WONDERFUL COMING-OF-AGE STORY!</em><p>In Betty Smith's classic, <strong>A Tree Grows In Brooklyn,</strong> she re-creates the challenging world of extreme poverty, starvation and hardship that she grew up in as a girl and teen in the Williamsburg slums from 1902-1919.</p><p>As an avid reader, it is hard <strong>not</strong> to fall in love with Francie, her love of books and the explosive excitement she <em>feels</em> from learning and attaining her goals. The more I became acquainted with the Nolan family, I just <strong>did not</strong> want the story to en</p></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6488433209914403781"><em>OH WHAT A WONDERFUL COMING-OF-AGE STORY!</em><p>In Betty Smith's classic, <strong>A Tree Grows In Brooklyn,</strong> she re-creates the challenging world of extreme poverty, starvation and hardship that she grew up in as a girl and teen in the Williamsburg slums from 1902-1919.</p><p>As an avid reader, it is hard <strong>not</strong> to fall in love with Francie, her love of books and the explosive excitement she <em>feels</em> from learning and attaining her goals. The more I became acquainted with the Nolan family, I just <strong>did not</strong> want the story to end.....the descriptive environment.....well-defined characters.....interesting jobs.......and oh my, what a penny or two could buy!</p><p>Yes, there are moments of sadness and despair, but also many uplifting family moments that made me laugh out loud. Between papa's shenanigans, crazy ole Aunt Sissy, and the disappointments of young love......not to mention Francie's encounter with the pervert.....I can only say, I don't know why I waited so long to read this memorable and entertaining classic.</p><p>Highly Recommend!</p></span>

          <a href="/book/show/456908.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780060929886" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who liked Little Women.</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer179319055">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6494236289787938412"><em>“Dear God," she prayed, "let me be something every minute of every hour of my life. Let me be gay; let me be sad. Let me be cold; let me be warm. Let me be hungry...have too much to eat. Let me be ragged or well dressed. Let me be sincere - be deceitful. Let me be truthful; let me be a liar. Let me be honorable and let me sin. Only let me be something every blessed minute. And when I sleep, let me dream all the time so that not one little piece of living is ever lost.” </em><br /><br />When I was just 10 pages i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6494236289787938412"><em>“Dear God," she prayed, "let me be something every minute of every hour of my life. Let me be gay; let me be sad. Let me be cold; let me be warm. Let me be hungry...have too much to eat. Let me be ragged or well dressed. Let me be sincere - be deceitful. Let me be truthful; let me be a liar. Let me be honorable and let me sin. Only let me be something every blessed minute. And when I sleep, let me dream all the time so that not one little piece of living is ever lost.” </em><br /><br />When I was just 10 pages into the book, I knew in my soul that Francie was one character that was going to stay with me for a long long time. Now, after 2 days of furious reading, not bothering about trifles like eating or picking up the cell phone, I can say with full conviction, that I will read this book again and again as long as I live, and I will remember about Francie whenever I indulge in self pity or start cribbing about life. Betty Smith, thank you for giving me Mary Francis Katherine Nolan, my literary soul sister.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6494236289787938412">...more</a>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16891593993504995996">This was a beautiful story about Francie and her poor family struggling through life in Brooklyn in the 1900s. It is told from Francie's point-of-view, but through the pages you feel a deep connection to the rest of the family as well, which just makes the story even more touching. <br />I especially loved how some of the sentences and paragraphs in this book were poignant because they were very true and honest. I often had to stop and reread a passage just because it hit spot on and described life perfectly.<br />This book also comes with one of the most perfect and satisfying endings I've encountered for a long time. Things wrap up beautifully and once again this underscores how life is really like and how we never really know what is coming. This is definitely worth a read! </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_14189336">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14189336" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/612636-beth" class="left imgcol" title="Beth"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1447971489p2/612636.jpg" alt="Beth" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14189336?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 20, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/612636-beth" class="user" title="Beth">Beth</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/372107.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Hardcover 9780060001940" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/612636-beth?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14189336">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18334094356620938631">I think my mom once said that this was her favorite book, and yet somehow I hadn’t read it until now. In my early teens, I remember coming across a paperback edition that had been lying around the house … and not making it past the first couple pages. The writing was way over my head (which had been addled by too much fluff reading of Baby-sitters Club, probably).<br /><br />It’s probably for the best, though, for while this book centers around the young girl Francie Nolan, this coming-of-age story is appro</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18334094356620938631">I think my mom once said that this was her favorite book, and yet somehow I hadn’t read it until now. In my early teens, I remember coming across a paperback edition that had been lying around the house … and not making it past the first couple pages. The writing was way over my head (which had been addled by too much fluff reading of Baby-sitters Club, probably).<br /><br />It’s probably for the best, though, for while this book centers around the young girl Francie Nolan, this coming-of-age story is appropriately shelved in the adult section — for some language (I really am such a prude when it comes to cursing, but while this book had it sprinkled liberally throughout, I didn’t find that it bothered me much — the writing was that good) and certainly for “thematic elements.”<br /><br />So. What is this book about? I couldn’t begin to summarize. The storyline is hardly even a cohesive story at all — not with a formulaic introduction-conflict-resolution, anyway. It’s more a series of varied vignettes, ranging from humorous to harrowing, wise to whimsical. It seemed like in every one of the fifty-some chapters, I’d find some paragraph that spoke to my soul. When I’d (finally) close the book for the day, I’d sigh contentedly, as though reading A Tree Grows in Brooklyn was like the pickle Francie enjoyed on page 45: “She didn’t exactly eat it. She just had it.” Yes, that was kind of like my reading — I didn’t exactly read it; I just absorbed it.<br /><br />Okay, so really: what is this book about? Well, it’s about a family in Brooklyn in the early twentieth century, trying to make ends meet in impoverished conditions — and trying to be happy in spite of their circumstances. The characters were very likable (which, for me, often translates to good, morally sound people — basically true in this case, despite some questionable habits) and yet simultaneously very real. They struggle throughout almost the entire course of the book, scraping up a penny or two for a tin-can bank — the hope for a better future for the children Francie and Neeley — while going hungry and cold in the meantime.<br /><br />Yet somehow, though they are destitute, you can’t but help feel these Nolans (and Rommelys — the mother’s family) are rich in the things that matter: in moral courage and in their relationships with one another … and, yes, in happiness. Francie wisely reflects on page 457:<br /><br /> “People always think that happiness is a faraway thing … something complicated and hard to get. Yet, what little things can make it up.”<br /><br />It’s all the little, uncomplicated things of this story that make it a happy one — a book worth reading … and one that will stay with me for some time, I’m sure.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18334094356620938631">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer9237601299826173027">A Tree Grows in Brooklyn is another classic that I've always heard about, but never read - until now! And it's another one that I wish I had read sooner! If someone told me that this was an autobiography, I would believe it. It was extremely believable and well-written. Betty Smith completely drew me into Francie Nolan's world. I feel like I was there and lived that life with her - which is amazing because my life and experiences are very different from Francie's world as a poor young girl growi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9237601299826173027">A Tree Grows in Brooklyn is another classic that I've always heard about, but never read - until now! And it's another one that I wish I had read sooner! If someone told me that this was an autobiography, I would believe it. It was extremely believable and well-written. Betty Smith completely drew me into Francie Nolan's world. I feel like I was there and lived that life with her - which is amazing because my life and experiences are very different from Francie's world as a poor young girl growing up in Brooklyn in the early 1900s. <br /><br />I read the bulk of this book in two sittings, but stretched out the last 100 pages over several days because I wasn't ready for it to end yet. This book fulfilled in a very wonderful way one of the reasons I love to read: experiencing someone else's life while going somewhere I would never be able to go on my own.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9237601299826173027">...more</a>

          <a href="/book/show/482976.A_Tree_Grows_in_Brooklyn" title="Paperback 9780060736262" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer226302322">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17403129834505839192">Another American classic finally read. I'm very glad to have finally experienced this book. It was really more than I expected, a wonderful story of a young girl growing up in early twentieth century Brooklyn with her parents and brother. The life was hard with family foremost but not perfect. The details were perfect, from the multiple uses for bread to stretch out meals to details from school experiences to conversations between mother and daughter revealing depths of honesty and past despair.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17403129834505839192">Another American classic finally read. I'm very glad to have finally experienced this book. It was really more than I expected, a wonderful story of a young girl growing up in early twentieth century Brooklyn with her parents and brother. The life was hard with family foremost but not perfect. The details were perfect, from the multiple uses for bread to stretch out meals to details from school experiences to conversations between mother and daughter revealing depths of honesty and past despair. <br /><br />So much happens in the six or seven years we follow Francie and her family in their lives. There are births, deaths, graduations, jobs won and lost, weddings and funerals, occasional laughter and more weeping. There is a lot of unspoken love between people who don't appear to know how to express their feelings for each other. <br /><br />This book had a personal impact on me because of the time period involved. My mother was born in the time frame of this story and would have experienced some of what Francie experienced in her life, though not in New York. It makes me wish to know more about my mother's life, things I never will know now.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15761776517405534925">Loved it! A fantastic coming of age story.<br /><br />Initial Thoughts:<br />1. Very relatable. Frances Nolan was born into a family that didn't have much, but managed to make it work every day. A great story of perseverance and determination.<br />2. Thoughtful and honest. I appreciated the realities Betty Smith brought forth, and the profound words that give strength to those who struggle.<br />3. Slow read. It's a book that I wanted to take my time with and absorb each experience Frances and her family was going through.<br />4. Flow of the story is great. From the birth of Frances Nolan, her parents' backstory, her brothers birth, and her time growing up in Brooklyn.<br />5. Loved the characters!
